---

Most recruiting rankings are based on having analyst assign star ratings to every player in the country and using some formula to add those ratings to arrive at a team score. Analysts may assume that players who sign with Alabama are good and give them 4 stars. Or analysts may want SEC schools to rank highly so that SEC fans will visit the website. Or maybe analysts just are not perfect evaluators of talent.

There is one group of people who have careers that depend on accurate evaluation of high school football players: college football coaches. How can the data about which schools offered scholarships to which players be used to rank recruiting classes?


## Degree of Difficulty
The simplest thing to do is to judge players based on the number of scholarship offers they have accrued. A player with 10 scholarship offers might be better than a player with 3 scholarship offers. But 3 big time offers is more impressive than 10 mediocre offers, so we need to account for quality of offers. Beating out Alabama is more impressive than beating out UAB, but how can we infer that without knowing anything about star ratings?

One approach is to use on-field performance. Top ranked teams get the best players, but some schools consistently outperform or underperform recruiting expectations. Translating on-field performance to recruiting performance is difficult and imperfect. Instead, we will only focus on recruiting data and treat recruiting success the same way on-field success is analyzed to create rankings.

If Alabama beats Auburn and UAB for a recruit then we will count that as two victories for Alabama: one over Auburn and one over UAB. Repeating this process for every recruit yields data that can be analyzed almost like regular game results. We will use the ELO system as the basis for our rankings because they are a simple way to account for wins, losses, and strength of schedule.

Applying an ELO ranking as if our data was exactly like regular games yields the following top 25, which is obviously imperfect:

## Incomplete Data

The main problem with recruiting is that teams do not offer scholarships to every player that they want. If SEC schools are making offers to a player in the Southeast then WAC schools are unlikely try to compete even if they would definitely want that player. Some schools offer lots of scholarships that they know will almost certainly be in vain, while other schools only offer if they think they have a good chance of landing the player.

Schools can choose not to play games that they know they are going to lose, so the data on losses needs to be treated very carefully. Not only does the school that makes lots of offers get penalized more than they should, but any school that beats them is affected.

We are going to create an additional ranking: how hard a school is to defeat. We create this ranking by a raw ELO approach. Beating a team that rarely offers scholarships is more impressive than beating a team that offers everyone they want.

One additional wrinkle to make the rankings more accurate is to weight some victories more heavily. Players might have dozens of offers but only serious interest from the handful of schools that have the best chance. The closest competitors should be weighted more heavily because they likely provide the best insight into differing recruiting abilities.

Applying these modifications yields the following top 25:
